<p>Between its mainline operation and subsidiaries, and as of March 2015, Delta employs nearly 80,000 people.<sup id="cite_ref-statsfacts_12-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-statsfacts-12">[12]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ed_Bastian" title="Ed Bastian">Ed Bastian</a> is the current Chief Executive Officer and has served in this position since May 2, 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Joanne_Smith" title="Joanne Smith">Joanne Smith</a> is Executive Vice President and Chief Human Resources Officer responsible for the oversight and support of personnel needs at Delta. She was appointed on October 1, 2014, replacing Mike Campbell.<sup id="cite_ref-WSJ_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ-135">[135]</a></sup></p><p>Delta's 14,500 mainline pilots are represented by the <a href="/wiki/Air_Line_Pilots_Association,_International" title="Air Line Pilots Association, International">Air Line Pilots Association, International</a> and are the union's largest pilot group.<sup id="cite_ref-WSJESTERL_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJESTERL-136">[136]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> The company's approximately 180 flight dispatchers are represented by the Professional Airline Flight Control Association (PAFCA).<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> Not counting the pilots and flight dispatchers, Delta is the only one of the five <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_airlines_in_North_America" title="List of largest airlines in North America">largest airlines in the United States</a>, and one of only two in the top 9 (the other being JetBlue), whose non-pilot USA domestic staff is entirely non-union.<sup id="cite_ref-WSJESTERL_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJESTERL-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DeltaAirLinesHQAtlantaGA.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/DeltaAirLinesHQAtlantaGA.jpg/220px-DeltaAirLinesHQAtlantaGA.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 165px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/DeltaAirLinesHQAtlantaGA.jpg/220px-DeltaAirLinesHQAtlantaGA.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="165" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DeltaAirLinesHQAtlantaGA.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Delta Air Lines headquarters in Atlanta</div></div></div>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Headquarters_and_offices">Headquarters and offices</span></h3>
<p>Delta's corporate headquarters is located on a corporate campus on the northern boundary of Hartsfield-Jackson Atlanta International Airport, within the city limits of Atlanta.<sup id="cite_ref-schmidt_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schmidt-139">[139]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> This location has served as Delta's headquarters since 1941, when the company relocated its corporate offices from <a href="/wiki/Monroe,_Louisiana" title="Monroe, Louisiana">Monroe, Louisiana</a> to <a href="/wiki/Greater_Atlanta" class="mw-redirect" title="Greater Atlanta">Greater Atlanta</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> The crop dusting division of Delta remained headquartered in Monroe until Delta ceased crop dusting in 1966.<sup id="cite_ref-journey_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-journey-1">[1]</a></sup> Before 1981, the Delta corporate campus, an 80-acre (32 ha) plot of land in proximity to the old Hartsfield Airport terminal, was outside the City of Atlanta limits in <a href="/wiki/Unincorporated_area" title="Unincorporated area">unincorporated</a> <a href="/wiki/Fulton_County,_Georgia" title="Fulton County, Georgia">Fulton County</a>. On August 3, 1981, the <a href="/wiki/Atlanta_City_Council" title="Atlanta City Council">Atlanta City Council</a> approved the annexation of 141 acres (57 ha) of land, an area containing the Delta headquarters. As of 1981 Delta would have had to begin paying $200,000 annually to the City of Atlanta in taxes. In September 1981, the airline sued the city, challenging the annexation on the basis of the constitutionality of the 1960 City of Atlanta annexation of the Hartsfield old terminal.<sup id="cite_ref-DeltaSuingCityATL_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeltaSuingCityATL-144">[144]</a></sup> The City of Atlanta was only permitted to annex areas that are adjacent to areas already in the Atlanta city limits.<sup id="cite_ref-DeltaSuingCityATL_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeltaSuingCityATL-144">[144]</a></sup></p><p>In addition to hosting Delta's corporate headquarters, Hartsfield-Jackson is also the home of <a href="/wiki/Delta_TechOps" title="Delta TechOps">Delta TechOps</a>, the airline's primary maintenance, repair, and overhaul arm and the largest full-service airline MRO in North America, specializing in engines, components, airframe, and line maintenance.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup></p><p>Delta maintains a large presence in the <a href="/wiki/Minneapolis_%E2%80%93_Saint_Paul" class="mw-redirect" title="Minneapolis – Saint Paul">Twin Cities</a>, with over 12,000 employees<sup id="cite_ref-mpr_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mpr-146">[146]</a></sup> in the region as well as significant corporate support functions housed in the Minneapolis area, including the company's information technology divisional offices.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Corporate_identity">Corporate identity</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Delta_Air_Lines_A330-323_(N809NW)_landing_at_Amsterdam_Airport_Schiphol_(1).jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Delta_Air_Lines_A330-323_%28N809NW%29_landing_at_Amsterdam_Airport_Schiphol_%281%29.jpg/220px-Delta_Air_Lines_A330-323_%28N809NW%29_landing_at_Amsterdam_Airport_Schiphol_%281%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 146px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Delta_Air_Lines_A330-323_%28N809NW%29_landing_at_Amsterdam_Airport_Schiphol_%281%29.jpg/220px-Delta_Air_Lines_A330-323_%28N809NW%29_landing_at_Amsterdam_Airport_Schiphol_%281%29.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="146" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Delta_Air_Lines_A330-323_(N809NW)_landing_at_Amsterdam_Airport_Schiphol_(1).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An Airbus A330-300 painted in Delta's current livery, "Upward &amp; Onward"</div></div></div>
<p>Delta's logo, often called the "widget", was originally unveiled in 1959. Its triangle shape is taken from the Greek letter <a href="/wiki/Delta_(letter)" title="Delta (letter)">delta</a>, and recalls the airline's origins in the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_Delta" title="Mississippi Delta">Mississippi Delta</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mouawad1_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mouawad1-148">[148]</a></sup> It is also said to be reminiscent of the swept-wing design of the DC-8, Delta's first jet aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup></p><p>Delta's current <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_livery" title="Aircraft livery">livery</a> is called "Upward &amp; Onward". It features a white fuselage with the company's name in blue lettering, and a widget on the vertical stabilizer. Delta introduced its current livery in 2007 as part of a re-branding after it emerged from bankruptcy. The new livery consists of four colors, while the old one (called "colors in motion") used eight. This meant the switch saved the airline money by removing one day from each aircraft's painting cycle. The airline took four years to repaint all of its aircraft into the current scheme, including aircraft inherited from Northwest Airlines.<sup id="cite_ref-Mouawad1_148-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mouawad1-148">[148]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_initiatives">Environmental initiatives</span></h3>
<p>In 2008, Delta Air Lines was given an award from the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">United States Environmental Protection Agency</a>'s Design for the Environment (DfE) program for their use of PreKote, a more environmentally friendly, non-hexavalent chromium surface pretreatment on its aircraft, replacing hazardous chemicals formerly used to improve paint adhesion and prevent corrosion. In addition, PreKote reduces water usage by two-thirds and reduces wastewater treatment.
</p><p>PreKote is also saving money by reducing the time needed to paint each airplane. With time savings of eight to ten percent, it will save an estimated more than $1 million annually.<sup id="cite_ref-Delta_Air_Lines_News_Release_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Delta_Air_Lines_News_Release-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(8)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</span></h2><section class="mf-section-8 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-8">
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Deltalina">Deltalina</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Deltalina" title="Deltalina">Deltalina</a></div>
<p>As part of the re-branding project, a <a href="/wiki/Safety_video" class="mw-redirect" title="Safety video">safety video</a> featuring a flight attendant showed up on YouTube in early 2008, getting over 1 million views and the attention of news outlets, specifically for the video's tone mixed with the serious safety message. The flight attendant, Katherine Lee, was dubbed "Deltalina" by a member of FlyerTalk for her resemblance to <a href="/wiki/Angelina_Jolie" title="Angelina Jolie">Angelina Jolie</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> Delta had considered several styles for its current safety video, including animation, before opting for a video presenting a flight attendant speaking to the audience. The video was filmed on a former Song Airlines Boeing 757-200.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(9)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Accidents_and_incidents">Accidents and incidents</span></h2><section class="mf-section-9 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-9">

The probable cause of the accident was wake turbulence resulting from a <a href="/wiki/Touch-and-go_landing" title="Touch-and-go landing">touch-and-go landing</a> moments before of American Airlines Flight 1114, operated using a <a href="/wiki/DC-10" class="mw-redirect" title="DC-10">DC-10</a>. The right wing hit the ground causing a fire resulting in the aircraft being written off.

One person on the ground was killed as the plane crossed a highway. The crash would later become the subject of a television movie. Numerous changes to pilot wind shear training, weather forecasting, and wind shear detection were made as a result of this crash.<sup id="cite_ref-changed_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-changed-167">[167]</a></sup></td>

</td></tr><tr><td>37<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup></td>
<td>July 8, 1987
</td>
<td>Lockheed L-1011
</td>
<td>North Atlantic Ocean
</td>
<td>Near collision with a <a href="/wiki/Continental_Airlines" title="Continental Airlines">Continental Airlines</a> 747 carrying 418 passengers and crew. Both the Delta (London-Cincinnati) and Continental (London-Newark) were heading to the U.S. with nearly 600 people total on both aircraft. The Delta flight strayed 60 miles (97 km) off course to the south from its assigned "C" track during its flight and came within 30 feet (9.1 m) of colliding with the 747 as the L-1011 flew under it in Canadian airspace, flying on the "D" track (there are five westbound and five eastbound tracks from the United States to the United Kingdom). Had the planes actually collided, it could have tied the <a href="/wiki/Tenerife_airport_disaster" title="Tenerife airport disaster">Tenerife airport disaster</a> as the deadliest aviation accident in history.

<td>While landing in heavy snowfall, Flight 1086 from Atlanta departed the left side of runway while traveling approximately 100 mph (160 km/h), coming to rest nearly perpendicular to the runway with its nose on an embankment beside the waters of <a href="/wiki/Flushing_Bay" title="Flushing Bay">Flushing Bay</a>. This resulted in substantial damage to the aircraft's radome (hail penetrating nose causing a large hole) as well as leading edges of the wing destroyed and damage to the tail plane.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> This aircraft was written off due to its age and because Delta had already began retiring its Boeing 747-400 fleet.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> Temporary repairs were made (to make it airworthy) and the aircraft was ferried from Seoul to <a href="/wiki/Pinal_Airpark" title="Pinal Airpark">Pinal Airpark</a> in Marana, Arizona.

<td>56
</td></tr></tbody></table><ul><li>The attempted bombing of <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Airlines_Flight_253" title="Northwest Airlines Flight 253">Northwest Airlines Flight 253</a> on December 25, 2009, occurred four days before the operating certificates of Northwest and Delta were combined (December 31, 2009). The aircraft involved in the incident was in Delta livery and reported in some early news reports as "Delta Flight 253."<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup></li></ul><h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Hijackings">Hijackings</span></h3>
<p>There have been over a dozen attempted hijackings that resulted in no injuries and the surrender of the often lone hijacker. These incidents are not included. The following are notable hijackings because of fatalities or success in forcing the aircraft to fly to another country:
</p>
<ul><li>On February 21, 1968, a Delta DC-8 was hijacked to Havana, Cuba. This was the first successful hijacking to Cuba from the U.S. since 1961,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and was the start of multiple hijacking attempts to Cuba in the late 1960s. This coincided with the introduction of passenger screening using metal detectors in U.S. airports starting in the late 1960s.</li>
<li>On July 31, 1972, <a href="/wiki/Delta_Flight_841" class="mw-redirect" title="Delta Flight 841">Delta Flight 841</a>, a Detroit to Miami DC-8 flight, was hijacked to <a href="/wiki/Algiers,_Algeria" class="mw-redirect" title="Algiers, Algeria">Algiers, Algeria</a> by 8 hijackers. The aircraft stopped in Boston to pick up an international navigator. The flight was allowed to return with passengers to the U.S., stopping in Barcelona for refueling.<sup id="cite_ref-nbc_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nbc-182">[182]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup></li>
<li>On February 22, 1974, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Byck" title="Samuel Byck">Samuel Byck</a>, an unemployed tire salesman from Pennsylvania, shot and killed an Aviation Police Officer, then stormed aboard <a href="/wiki/Delta_Air_Lines_Flight_523" class="mw-redirect" title="Delta Air Lines Flight 523">Delta Air Lines Flight 523</a>, a McDonnell Douglas DC-9 flight at Baltimore Friendship Airport (now <a href="/wiki/Baltimore%E2%80%93Washington_International_Airport" title="Baltimore–Washington International Airport">Baltimore–Washington International Airport</a>) scheduled to fly to Atlanta, and shot both pilots, killing the First Officer, Fred Jones. He intended to crash the plane into the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-schneier_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schneier-184">[184]</a></sup> After shooting the pilots, the hijacker grabbed a passenger and demanded that she fly the aircraft.<sup id="cite_ref-cbs_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cbs-185">[185]</a></sup> The passengers meanwhile fled the aircraft. The hijacking attempt ended when, after a standoff with police, Byck was shot twice through an aircraft porthole by a Maryland policeman, severely wounding him. Before police stormed the plane, Byck killed himself. The plane never left the gate during this incident.</li>
<li>On August 23, 1980, a Delta Air Lines L-1011 on a San Juan to Los Angeles flight was hijacked and flown to Cuba.<sup id="cite_ref-time_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-time-186">[186]</a></sup> The hijacker was jailed by Cuban authorities, and all passengers were released unharmed.</li>
<li>On September 13, 1980, a Delta Air Lines New Orleans to Atlanta flight was taken over by two hijackers and forced to fly to Cuba. The flight continued to Atlanta after stopping in Havana. The hijackers were imprisoned by Cuban authorities. One hijacker was released and later sought US residency. The suspect was later arrested by US authorities in 2002<sup id="cite_ref-diaz_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-diaz-187">[187]</a></sup> and sentenced to 10 years in prison the following year.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup></li>
<li>Additional hijackings that resulted in no injuries and the flight landing in Cuba include March 28, 1984 (Delta 357, a 727, New Orleans-Dallas),<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> August 18, 1983 (Delta 784, a 727, Miami-Tampa),<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup> July 17, 1983 (Delta 722, a 727, Miami-Tampa),<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> and June 11, 1979 (Delta 1061, a L-1011, New York LaGuardia-Fort Lauderdale).<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup></li></ul></section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(10)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2><section class="mf-section-10 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-10">
